# Searching Sourcegraph

Search before you build. Existing patterns reduce tokens, ensure consistency, and surface tested solutions.

Use `sg_list_repos` to discover available repositories before searching.


## Tool Selection Logic

**Start here:**

1. **Know the exact symbol or pattern?** → `sg_keyword_search`
2. **Know the concept, not the code?** → `sg_nls_search`
3. **Need to understand how/why?** → `sg_deepsearch_read`
4. **Tracing a symbol's usage?** → `sg_find_references`
5. **Need full implementation?** → `sg_go_to_definition` → `sg_read_file`

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Exact code patterns | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Trace usage | `sg_find_references` |
| See implementation | `sg_go_to_definition` |
| Understand systems | `sg_deepsearch_read` |
| Read files | `sg_read_file` |
| Browse structure | `sg_list_files` |
| Find repos | `sg_list_repos` |
| Search commits | `sg_commit_search` |
| Track changes | `sg_diff_search` |
| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
file:.*\.test\.ts$ -file:__mocks__   # Tests, exclude mocks
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

Combine filters: `repo:^github.com/myorg/backend$ file:src/handlers lang:typescript`

## Context-Aware Behaviour

**When the user provides a file path or error message:**
- Extract symbols, function names, or error codes
- Search for those exact terms first
- Trace references if the error involves a known symbol

**When the user asks "how does X work":**
- Prefer `sg_deepsearch_read` for architectural understanding
- Follow up with `sg_read_file` on key files mentioned in the response

**When the user is implementing a new feature:**
- Search for similar existing implementations first
- Read tests for usage examples
- Check for shared utilities before creating new ones

**When debugging:**
- Search for the exact error string
- Trace the symbol where the error is thrown
- Check recent changes with `sg_diff_search`

## Workflows

For detailed step-by-step workflows, see:
- `workflows/implementing-feature.md` — when building new features
- `workflows/understanding-code.md` — when exploring unfamiliar systems
- `workflows/debugging-issue.md` — when tracking down bugs

## Efficiency Rules

**Minimise tool calls:**
- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms (faster, more precise)

**Batch your understanding:**
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than reading one and asking questions
- Use `sg_deepsearch_read` for "how does X work" instead of multiple keyword searches

**Avoid common token waste:**
- Don't search all repos when you know the target repo
- Don't use `sg_deepsearch_read` for simple "find all" queries
- Don't re-read files you've already seen in this conversation

## Query Patterns

| Intent | Query |
|--------|-------|
| React hooks | `file:.*\.tsx$ use[A-Z].*= \(` |
| API routes | `file:src/api app\.(get\|post\|put\|delete)` |
| Error handling | `catch.*Error\|\.catch\(` |
| Type definitions | `file:types/ export (interface\|type)` |
| Test setup | `file:.*\.test\. beforeEach\|beforeAll` |
| Config files | `file:(webpack\|vite\|rollup)\.config` |
| CI/CD | `file:\.github/workflows deploy` |

For more patterns, see `query-patterns.md`.

## Output Formatting

**Search results:**
- Present as a brief summary, not raw tool output
- Highlight the most relevant file and line
- Include a code snippet only if it directly answers the question

**Code explanations:**
- Start with a one-sentence summary
- Use the codebase's own terminology
- Reference specific files and functions

**Recommendations:**
- Present as numbered steps if actionable
- Link to specific patterns found in the codebase
- Note any existing utilities that should be reused

## Common Mistakes

| Mistake | Fix |
|---------|-----|
| Searching all repos | Add `repo:^github.com/org/repo$` |
| Too many results | Add `file:` pattern or keywords |
| Missing relevant code | Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching |
| Not understanding context | Use `sg_deepsearch_read` |
| Guessing patterns | Read implementations with `sg_read_file` |

## Principles

- Start narrow, expand if needed
- Chain tools: search → read → find references → definition
- Check tests for usage examples
- Read before generating

# Task: Kubernetes API Server Architecture and Extension Guide

**Repository:** kubernetes/kubernetes (stripped snapshot)
**Output:** Write your document to `/workspace/documentation.md`

## Objective

Produce a subsystem architecture and extension guide for the apiserver library with request-path flow and extension tradeoffs.

## Scope

Focus on the Kubernetes apiserver library subsystem.
Your document must explain component responsibilities, end-to-end flow, and extension or operational tradeoffs.

## Content Expectations

Address all of the following in your own structure:
- subsystem purpose, boundaries, and upstream/downstream dependencies
- key components and how responsibilities are split
- end-to-end control/request flow with concrete repository evidence
- failure modes, limits, and design tradeoffs
- extension points, customization hooks, and associated risks
- a concise map of the most important files/directories used in your analysis

## Quality Bar

- Cite concrete file paths from the repository.
- Explain behavior and interactions, not just API signatures.
- Include at least one section on operational implications (performance, reliability, or maintainability).
- Do not fabricate classes, functions, or files.

## Anti-Requirements

- Do not output `doc.go`.
- Do not provide shallow bullet dumps without system flow explanation.
- Do not rely on external docs not present in the workspace.

## Document Summary

**`/workspace/documentation.md`** is a 13-section architecture and extension guide covering:

1. **Purpose and Boundaries** — what the library owns vs. its upstream (`apimachinery`, `client-go`, etcd) and downstream consumers (`kube-apiserver`, `kube-aggregator`, `apiextensions-apiserver`).

2. **Server Composition: The Delegation Chain** — the three-`GenericAPIServer` chain assembled in `cmd/kube-apiserver/app/server.go`, linked via the `DelegationTarget` interface (`genericapiserver.go:312`), terminating at `emptyDelegate`.

3. **Configuration Pipeline** — the mandatory `Config → Complete() → completedConfig.New()` staged construction (`config.go:112–779`), including all significant tunable fields with their defaults.

4. **The HTTP Handler Chain** — the complete ordered list of filters from `DefaultBuildHandlerChain` (`config.go:1028–1109`), explaining why auth happens before body parsing and how `BuildHandlerChainFunc` enables replacement.

5. **API Group Registration** — `APIGroupInfo`, the `APIInstaller.Install()` loop (`installer.go:195`), interface-based verb capability detection, and the `runtime.Scheme` as type/conversion/defaulting registry.

6. **`rest.Storage` and `genericregistry.Store`** — the interface capability hierarchy (`rest/rest.go`), the `Store` struct's strategy hooks (`store.go:172–248`), and `DryRunnableStorage`.

7. **Storage Stack** — the three-layer path from `Store` → `Cacher` → `etcd3.store`, the `storage.Interface` contract, watch cache initialization/serving/bookmark mechanics, and encryption transformer injection.

  "content": "{\"content\":\"1: # apiserver Architecture\\n2: \\n3: ## 1. Server Composition\\n4: \\n5: The `kube-apiserver` binary is not one server, but a **server chain** of three distinct\\n6: `GenericAPIServer` instances: the core Kubernetes API, API extensions (CRDs), and the aggregation\\n7: layer.\\n8: \\n9: This composition is managed by a layered configuration system, starting with command-line\\n10: flags parsed by `options` structs (e.g., `RecommendedOptions` in `pkg/server/options`), which\\n11: populate a `Config` object that is then used to instantiate the `GenericAPIServer`\\n12: instances. The construction of this delegation chain can be found in the `CreateServerChain`\\n13: function in `cmd/kube-apiserver/app/server.go`.\\n14: \\n15: ```mermaid\\n16: graph TD\\n17:     subgraph Incoming Request\\n18:         direction LR\\n19:         A[User/Client] --> B{/apis/apps/v1/deployments};\\n20:     end\\n21: \\n22:     subgraph kube-apiserver process\\n23:         direction LR\\n24:         B --> C[Aggregator Server];\\n25:         C -- Not an APIService --> D[Kube API Server];\\n26:         D -- Handles Request --> E[REST Storage];\\n27:         C -- Is an APIService --> F[Proxy to Extension API Server];\\n28:         D -- Not a Core API --> G[API Extensions Server];\\n29:         G -- Handles CRD --> E;\\n30:     end\\n31: ```\\n32: \\n33: 1.  **Aggregator Server (`kube-aggregator`):**\\n34:     *   **Purpose:** Handles the `apiregistration.k8s.io` API and acts as a reverse proxy for\\n35:         extension API servers. This functionality was designed to allow third-party APIs to be\\n36:         \\\"aggregated\\\" into the main Kubernetes API server seamlessly.\\n37:     *   **Mechanism:** It watches `APIService` objects. When a request arrives (e.g., for\\n38:         `/apis/mycompany.com/v1/myresources`), it checks if an `APIService` has \\\"claimed\\\"\\n39:         that path. If so, it uses a `ServiceResolver` to find the IP of the backing\\n40:         `Service` and proxies the request.\\n41:     *   **Use Case:** This pattern is for programmatic, high-control extensions that require\\n42:         custom business logic (e.g., non-CRUD subresources like `/logs`) or alternative\\n43:         storage backends.\\n44:     *   **Delegation:** If no `APIService` matches, it delegates the request to the next\\n45:         server in the chain.\\n46: \\n47: 2.  **Kube API Server (Core):**\\n48:     *   **Purpose:** Serves all the built-in Kubernetes APIs (`core/v1`, `apps/v1`, etc.).\\n49:     *   **Mechanism:** This is the main server, configured with all the core REST storage\\n50:         strategies.\\n51:     *   **Delegation:** If a request is for a path that is not a core API (e.g., for a CRD),\\n52:         it delegates the request to the next server in the chain.\\n53: \\n54: 3.  **API Extensions Server (`apiextensions-apiserver`):**\\n55:     *   **Purpose:** Handles the `apiextensions.k8s.io` API, which manages\\n56:         `CustomResourceDefinition` objects (CRDs). The evolution of CRDs from a simple extension\\n57:         mechanism to a feature-rich system with validation, versioning, and defaulting is\\n58:         documented in a series of KEPs, starting with the graduation to GA in Kubernetes v1.16.\\n59:     *   **Mechanism:** When a CRD is created, this server dynamically creates and installs a\\n60:         new REST storage handler for the new resource, making it immediately available.\\n61:     *   **Use Case:** CRDs are the most common extension pattern, offering a declarative,\\n62:         schema-based way to define new resource types that are stored in etcd and require no\\n63:         custom API server code.\\n64:     *   **Delegation:** It is the end of the chain. If it cannot handle a request, a `404 Not\\n65:         Found` is returned.\\n66: \\n67: ## 2. Handler Chain\\n68: \\n69: Every request flows through a standard chain of HTTP handlers (filters). The request body\\n70: is not deserialized until it has passed authentication and authorization. The default handler\\n71: chain is constructed by the `DefaultBuildHandlerChain` function in\\n72: `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/config.go`.\\n73: \\n74: ```mermaid\\n75: sequenceDiagram\\n76:     participant Client\\n77:     participant Handler Chain\\n78:     participant Authentication\\n79:     participant Authorization\\n80:     participant Priority and Fairness\\n81:     participant Admission Control\\n82:     participant REST Endpoint\\n83: \\n84:     Client->>Handler Chain: Request\\n85:     Handler Chain->>Authentication: Authenticate\\n86:     Authentication-->>Handler Chain: User Info\\n87:     Handler Chain->>Authorization: Authorize\\n88:     Authorization-->>Handler Chain: Allowed/Denied\\n89:     Handler Chain->>Priority and Fairness: Classify & Queue\\n90:     Priority and Fairness-->>Handler Chain: Proceed\\n91:     Handler Chain->>Admission Control: Mutate & Validate\\n92:     Admission Control-->>Handler Chain: Object OK\\n93:     Handler Chain->>REST Endpoint: Handle\\n94:     REST Endpoint-->>Handler Chain: Response\\n95:     Handler Chain-->>Client: Response\\n96: ```\\n97: \\n98: The handler chain consists of the following stages:\\n99: \\n100: 1.  **Authentication (`pkg/authentication`):** This filter identifies the user. The system is\\n101:     pluggable and composed of multiple authenticators (e.g., client certs, bearer tokens, OIDC).\\n102:     The identity of the user is determined by the first authenticator in the chain that successfully\\n103:     identifies the user.\\n104: 2.  **Authorization (`pkg/authorization`):** This filter checks if the user is permitted to\\n105:     perform the action. This system is also pluggable and composed of multiple authorizers\\n106:     (e.g., RBAC, Node, Webhook). Each authorizer may respond with either allow, deny, or no opinion.\\n107:     If the response is no opinion, the request is passed to the next authorizer in the chain.\\n108: 3.  **Priority and Fairness (`pkg/util/flowcontrol`):** This subsystem manages request\\n109:     concurrency, classifying requests into `FlowSchema`s and `PriorityLevel`s to prevent\\n110:     overload. This feature was introduced to prevent high traffic from overwhelming the API\\n111:     server and to ensure that critical cluster operations are not starved.\\n112: 4.  **Admission Control (`pkg/admission`):** This is the primary mechanism for policy\\n113:     enforcement. It is only at this stage that the request body is deserialized into an\\n114:     object. It is a chain of plugins that can mutate or validate an object. The built-in Pod\\n115:     Security admission controller is a key example of this, enforcing Pod Security Standards\\n116:     at the namespace level.\\n117: 5.  **REST Endpoint Handling (`pkg/endpoints`):** The request is finally dispatched to the\\n118:     appropriate REST handler, which is installed by the `APIInstaller`.\\n119: \\n120: ## 3. API Group Registration\\n121: \\n122: The high-level steps for introducing an API are:\\n123: \\n124: 1.  **Define Types:** Create or modify the Go structs in the `types.go` file for the API group.\\n125: 2.  **Generate Code:** Use the code generators provided by the Kubernetes project to create the required\\n126:     boilerplate methods for deep-copy, conversion, and defaulting.\\n127: 3.  **Implement the `Strategy`:** Write the custom business logic and validation for the\\n128:     resource in its `Strategy` object.\\n129: 4.  **Register and Install:** Create the `APIGroupInfo` struct, bundling the `Scheme` and the\\n130:     `Strategy`-configured storage, and pass it to the `GenericAPIServer`'s `InstallAPIGroup`\\n131:     method.\\n132: \\n133: ### The API Group Registry\\n134: \\n135: The `runtime.Scheme` acts as a central registry for an API group's type information. A single\\n136: `Scheme` object is created for each API group and is responsible for the following key\\n137: capabilities:\\n138: \\n139: *   **Type Registration and Mapping:** The `Scheme`'s primary role is to map a GroupVersionKind\\n140:     (GVK) to its corresponding Go type and back. This process also relies on the `deepcopy-gen`\\n141:     tool to create `DeepCopy()` methods for each type, which is critical for ensuring that\\n142:     objects returned from caches are never modified directly.\\n143: \\n144: *   **API Conversion:** The `Scheme` stores the conversion functions that translate objects\\n145:     between different API versions. These functions are typically generated by the\\n146:     `conversion-gen` tool and enable the **hub-and-spoke** model.\\n147: \\n148: *   **Defaulting:** The `Scheme` registers defaulting functions that populate optional fields in\\n149:     an object. These are usually generated by the `defaulter-gen` tool.\\n150: \\n151: *   **Declarative Validation:** The `Scheme` can store and execute code-generated validation\\n152:     functions, providing a baseline level of validation. This is distinct from the primary,\\n153:     handwritten business logic validation, which is handled by the `Strategy` object.\\n154: \\n155: ### The `APIGroupInfo` Struct and `Strategy` Object\\n156: \\n157: With a populated `Scheme`, the API group is registered with the `GenericAPIServer` by bundling\\n158: the `Scheme` with the storage backend and versioning information into an `APIGroupInfo` struct.\\n159: \\n160: ```mermaid\\n161: graph TD\\n162:     subgraph Server Configuration\\n163:         A[APIGroupInfo for apps v1];\\n164:         A --> B{Scheme: Knows Deployment v1};\\n165:         A --> C{Storage: deployments RESTStorage};\\n166:         A --> D{Version Priority: v1, v1beta1};\\n167:     end\\n168: \\n169:     subgraph RESTStorage Implementation\\n170:         C --> E[genericregistry.Store];\\n171:         E --> F[etcd client];\\n172:         E --> G[Deployment Strategy];\\n173:     end\\n174: \\n175:     subgraph Server Runtime\\n176:         H[GenericAPIServer] -- InstallAPIGroup --> I[APIInstaller];\\n177:         I -- Uses --> A;\\n178:         I --> J{Register /apis/apps/v1/deployments};\\n179:         J --> K[HTTP Handler];\\n180:         K -- On Request --> C;\\n181:     end\\n182: ```\\n183: \\n184: The registration process follows these steps:\\n185: \\n186: 1.  **`APIGroupInfo` Construction:** For each API group, an `APIGroupInfo` struct is created,\\n187:     which contains the populated `Scheme`, a map of resources to their storage\\n188:     implementations, and an ordered list of **Version Priority**.\\n189: \\n190: 2.  **REST Storage Instantiation:** For each resource, a `genericregistry.Store` is created. It\\n191:     is configured with a resource-specific `Strategy` object that contains the core business\\n192:     logic (e.g., handwritten validation).\\n193: \\n194: 3.  **API Group Installation:** The `GenericAPIServer`'s `InstallAPIGroup` method takes the\\n195:     `APIGroupInfo` and uses an `APIInstaller` to expose the resources as HTTP endpoints.\\n196: \\n197: ## 4. Watch Cache\\n198: \\n199: To handle the high volume of watch requests from controllers without overwhelming etcd, the\\n200: apiserver uses a **watch cache**. The implementation can be found in\\n201: `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/storage/cacher/`.\\n202: \\n203: *   **Initialization:** The cacher first performs a `LIST` to get the current state of all\\n204:     objects and a `ResourceVersion` for that point-in-time. It then starts a `WATCH` from\\n205:     that version to ensure a consistent stream of events.\\n206: *   **Serving from Cache:** Most list and watch requests are served from this in-memory cache, which\\n207:     dramatically reduces the load on etcd. Consistent reads are also served from the\\n208:     cache. This is achieved by first fetching the revision number of the latest write from\\n209:     etcd. The server then ensures the cache is at least that recent\u2014waiting for it to\\n210:     refresh if necessary\u2014before serving the request.\\n211: *   **Fallback to Storage:** If a client request cannot be served from the\\n212:     cache's buffer, the request \\\"falls through\\\" to the underlying etcd storage.\\n213: *   **Bookmarks:** The cacher uses bookmark events to track the latest `ResourceVersion` for\\n214:     unchanged objects. This prevents the cache's `ResourceVersion` from becoming too old,\\n215:     which avoids the need for expensive relist operations from etcd when the objects have\\n216:     not been modified.\\n217: \\n218: ## 5. Conflict Resolution\\n219: \\n220: *   **Optimistic Concurrency via `resourceVersion`:** Clients are expected to perform updates using a\\n221:     read-modify-write workflow. The apiserver uses the `resourceVersion` field of every\\n222:     object to enforce optimistic concurrency. This `resourceVersion` is not an arbitrary number;\\n223:     it maps directly to etcd's globally consistent `mod_revision`. When a client submits an\\n224:     update (`PUT` or `PATCH`), it must provide the `resourceVersion` of the object it based its\\n225:     modifications on. If the `resourceVersion` on the server does not match the current\\n226:     `mod_revision` in etcd, the server rejects the request with a `409 Conflict` error. This\\n227:     forces the client to re-read the object, resolve the conflict, and resubmit with the new\\n228:     `resourceVersion`.\\n229: *   **Server-Side Apply:** A declarative, \\\"intent-based\\\" patch. The server maintains a\\n230:     `managedFields` section in the object's metadata to track which \\\"manager\\\" (e.g., a\\n231:     controller) owns each field. This allows multiple actors to manage different parts of the\\n232:     same object without overwriting each other's changes.\\n233: \\n234: ## 6. Discovery and OpenAPI\\n235: \\n236: Apiservers serve the `/apis` discovery endpoints and the `/openapi/v2` and `/openapi/v3`\\n237: specifications. The generation of the OpenAPI specification is a multi-stage process.\\n238: \\n239: *   **`openapi-gen`**: This tool reflects on Go structs, reads godoc comments, and looks at\\n240:     validation struct tags to generate a map of all API definitions.\\n241: *   **`zz_generated.openapi.go`**: The output is a large Go file containing a\\n242:     `GetOpenAPIDefinitions` function.\\n243: *   **Runtime**: The `GenericAPIServer` calls this generated function to build the final OpenAPI\\n244:     JSON spec that it serves to clients.\\n245: \\n246: ## 7. Security & Observability\\n247: \\n248: *   **Audit (`pkg/audit`):** The apiserver has a policy-driven event logging pipeline. The audit\\n249:     policy controls what is logged and at which stage of a request.\\n250: *   **Security:**\\n251:     *   **mTLS:** The primary authentication mechanism for system components.\\n252:     *   **Service Account Token Issuance:** The `kube-apiserver` acts as an OIDC provider,\\n253:         issuing and validating JWTs for `ServiceAccount`s.\\n254: \\n255: ## 8. Streaming Protocols\\n256: \\n257: *   **Websockets:** The apiserver uses websockets to upgrade HTTP\\n258:     connections for interactive, streaming protocols like `exec`, `attach`, and\\n259:     `port-forward`. The `UpgradeAwareProxyHandler` manages this process.\\n260: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/kubernetes/kubernetes@d820c046f5c520aab51ec850b263524b1fa5a4e9/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/ARCHITECTURE.md\"}",
  "structuredContent": {
    "content": "1: # apiserver Architecture\n2: \n3: ## 1. Server Composition\n4: \n5: The `kube-apiserver` binary is not one server, but a **server chain** of three distinct\n6: `GenericAPIServer` instances: the core Kubernetes API, API extensions (CRDs), and the aggregation\n7: layer.\n8: \n9: This composition is managed by a layered configuration system, starting with command-line\n10: flags parsed by `options` structs (e.g., `RecommendedOptions` in `pkg/server/options`), which\n11: populate a `Config` object that is then used to instantiate the `GenericAPIServer`\n12: instances. The construction of this delegation chain can be found in the `CreateServerChain`\n13: function in `cmd/kube-apiserver/app/server.go`.\n14: \n15: ```mermaid\n16: graph TD\n17:     subgraph Incoming Request\n18:         direction LR\n19:         A[User/Client] --> B{/apis/apps/v1/deployments};\n20:     end\n21: \n22:     subgraph kube-apiserver process\n23:         direction LR\n24:         B --> C[Aggregator Server];\n25:         C -- Not an APIService --> D[Kube API Server];\n26:         D -- Handles Request --> E[REST Storage];\n27:         C -- Is an APIService --> F[Proxy to Extension API Server];\n28:         D -- Not a Core API --> G[API Extensions Server];\n29:         G -- Handles CRD --> E;\n30:     end\n31: ```\n32: \n33: 1.  **Aggregator Server (`kube-aggregator`):**\n34:     *   **Purpose:** Handles the `apiregistration.k8s.io` API and acts as a reverse proxy for\n35:         extension API servers. This functionality was designed to allow third-party APIs to be\n36:         \"aggregated\" into the main Kubernetes API server seamlessly.\n37:     *   **Mechanism:** It watches `APIService` objects. When a request arrives (e.g., for\n38:         `/apis/mycompany.com/v1/myresources`), it checks if an `APIService` has \"claimed\"\n39:         that path. If so, it uses a `ServiceResolver` to find the IP of the backing\n40:         `Service` and proxies the request.\n41:     *   **Use Case:** This pattern is for programmatic, high-control extensions that require\n42:         custom business logic (e.g., non-CRUD subresources like `/logs`) or alternative\n43:         storage backends.\n44:     *   **Delegation:** If no `APIService` matches, it delegates the request to the next\n45:         server in the chain.\n46: \n47: 2.  **Kube API Server (Core):**\n48:     *   **Purpose:** Serves all the built-in Kubernetes APIs (`core/v1`, `apps/v1`, etc.).\n49:     *   **Mechanism:** This is the main server, configured with all the core REST storage\n50:         strategies.\n51:     *   **Delegation:** If a request is for a path that is not a core API (e.g., for a CRD),\n52:         it delegates the request to the next server in the chain.\n53: \n54: 3.  **API Extensions Server (`apiextensions-apiserver`):**\n55:     *   **Purpose:** Handles the `apiextensions.k8s.io` API, which manages\n56:         `CustomResourceDefinition` objects (CRDs). The evolution of CRDs from a simple extension\n57:         mechanism to a feature-rich system with validation, versioning, and defaulting is\n58:         documented in a series of KEPs, starting with the graduation to GA in Kubernetes v1.16.\n59:     *   **Mechanism:** When a CRD is created, this server dynamically creates and installs a\n60:         new REST storage handler for the new resource, making it immediately available.\n61:     *   **Use Case:** CRDs are the most common extension pattern, offering a declarative,\n62:         schema-based way to define new resource types that are stored in etcd and require no\n63:         custom API server code.\n64:     *   **Delegation:** It is the end of the chain. If it cannot handle a request, a `404 Not\n65:         Found` is returned.\n66: \n67: ## 2. Handler Chain\n68: \n69: Every request flows through a standard chain of HTTP handlers (filters). The request body\n70: is not deserialized until it has passed authentication and authorization. The default handler\n71: chain is constructed by the `DefaultBuildHandlerChain` function in\n72: `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/config.go`.\n73: \n74: ```mermaid\n75: sequenceDiagram\n76:     participant Client\n77:     participant Handler Chain\n78:     participant Authentication\n79:     participant Authorization\n80:     participant Priority and Fairness\n81:     participant Admission Control\n82:     participant REST Endpoint\n83: \n84:     Client->>Handler Chain: Request\n85:     Handler Chain->>Authentication: Authenticate\n86:     Authentication-->>Handler Chain: User Info\n87:     Handler Chain->>Authorization: Authorize\n88:     Authorization-->>Handler Chain: Allowed/Denied\n89:     Handler Chain->>Priority and Fairness: Classify & Queue\n90:     Priority and Fairness-->>Handler Chain: Proceed\n91:     Handler Chain->>Admission Control: Mutate & Validate\n92:     Admission Control-->>Handler Chain: Object OK\n93:     Handler Chain->>REST Endpoint: Handle\n94:     REST Endpoint-->>Handler Chain: Response\n95:     Handler Chain-->>Client: Response\n96: ```\n97: \n98: The handler chain consists of the following stages:\n99: \n100: 1.  **Authentication (`pkg/authentication`):** This filter identifies the user. The system is\n101:     pluggable and composed of multiple authenticators (e.g., client certs, bearer tokens, OIDC).\n102:     The identity of the user is determined by the first authenticator in the chain that successfully\n103:     identifies the user.\n104: 2.  **Authorization (`pkg/authorization`):** This filter checks if the user is permitted to\n105:     perform the action. This system is also pluggable and composed of multiple authorizers\n106:     (e.g., RBAC, Node, Webhook). Each authorizer may respond with either allow, deny, or no opinion.\n107:     If the response is no opinion, the request is passed to the next authorizer in the chain.\n108: 3.  **Priority and Fairness (`pkg/util/flowcontrol`):** This subsystem manages request\n109:     concurrency, classifying requests into `FlowSchema`s and `PriorityLevel`s to prevent\n110:     overload. This feature was introduced to prevent high traffic from overwhelming the API\n111:     server and to ensure that critical cluster operations are not starved.\n112: 4.  **Admission Control (`pkg/admission`):** This is the primary mechanism for policy\n113:     enforcement. It is only at this stage that the request body is deserialized into an\n114:     object. It is a chain of plugins that can mutate or validate an object. The built-in Pod\n115:     Security admission controller is a key example of this, enforcing Pod Security Standards\n116:     at the namespace level.\n117: 5.  **REST Endpoint Handling (`pkg/endpoints`):** The request is finally dispatched to the\n118:     appropriate REST handler, which is installed by the `APIInstaller`.\n119: \n120: ## 3. API Group Registration\n121: \n122: The high-level steps for introducing an API are:\n123: \n124: 1.  **Define Types:** Create or modify the Go structs in the `types.go` file for the API group.\n125: 2.  **Generate Code:** Use the code generators provided by the Kubernetes project to create the required\n126:     boilerplate methods for deep-copy, conversion, and defaulting.\n127: 3.  **Implement the `Strategy`:** Write the custom business logic and validation for the\n128:     resource in its `Strategy` object.\n129: 4.  **Register and Install:** Create the `APIGroupInfo` struct, bundling the `Scheme` and the\n130:     `Strategy`-configured storage, and pass it to the `GenericAPIServer`'s `InstallAPIGroup`\n131:     method.\n132: \n133: ### The API Group Registry\n134: \n135: The `runtime.Scheme` acts as a central registry for an API group's type information. A single\n136: `Scheme` object is created for each API group and is responsible for the following key\n137: capabilities:\n138: \n139: *   **Type Registration and Mapping:** The `Scheme`'s primary role is to map a GroupVersionKind\n140:     (GVK) to its corresponding Go type and back. This process also relies on the `deepcopy-gen`\n141:     tool to create `DeepCopy()` methods for each type, which is critical for ensuring that\n142:     objects returned from caches are never modified directly.\n143: \n144: *   **API Conversion:** The `Scheme` stores the conversion functions that translate objects\n145:     between different API versions. These functions are typically generated by the\n146:     `conversion-gen` tool and enable the **hub-and-spoke** model.\n147: \n148: *   **Defaulting:** The `Scheme` registers defaulting functions that populate optional fields in\n149:     an object. These are usually generated by the `defaulter-gen` tool.\n150: \n151: *   **Declarative Validation:** The `Scheme` can store and execute code-generated validation\n152:     functions, providing a baseline level of validation. This is distinct from the primary,\n153:     handwritten business logic validation, which is handled by the `Strategy` object.\n154: \n155: ### The `APIGroupInfo` Struct and `Strategy` Object\n156: \n157: With a populated `Scheme`, the API group is registered with the `GenericAPIServer` by bundling\n158: the `Scheme` with the storage backend and versioning information into an `APIGroupInfo` struct.\n159: \n160: ```mermaid\n161: graph TD\n162:     subgraph Server Configuration\n163:         A[APIGroupInfo for apps v1];\n164:         A --> B{Scheme: Knows Deployment v1};\n165:         A --> C{Storage: deployments RESTStorage};\n166:         A --> D{Version Priority: v1, v1beta1};\n167:     end\n168: \n169:     subgraph RESTStorage Implementation\n170:         C --> E[genericregistry.Store];\n171:         E --> F[etcd client];\n172:         E --> G[Deployment Strategy];\n173:     end\n174: \n175:     subgraph Server Runtime\n176:         H[GenericAPIServer] -- InstallAPIGroup --> I[APIInstaller];\n177:         I -- Uses --> A;\n178:         I --> J{Register /apis/apps/v1/deployments};\n179:         J --> K[HTTP Handler];\n180:         K -- On Request --> C;\n181:     end\n182: ```\n183: \n184: The registration process follows these steps:\n185: \n186: 1.  **`APIGroupInfo` Construction:** For each API group, an `APIGroupInfo` struct is created,\n187:     which contains the populated `Scheme`, a map of resources to their storage\n188:     implementations, and an ordered list of **Version Priority**.\n189: \n190: 2.  **REST Storage Instantiation:** For each resource, a `genericregistry.Store` is created. It\n191:     is configured with a resource-specific `Strategy` object that contains the core business\n192:     logic (e.g., handwritten validation).\n193: \n194: 3.  **API Group Installation:** The `GenericAPIServer`'s `InstallAPIGroup` method takes the\n195:     `APIGroupInfo` and uses an `APIInstaller` to expose the resources as HTTP endpoints.\n196: \n197: ## 4. Watch Cache\n198: \n199: To handle the high volume of watch requests from controllers without overwhelming etcd, the\n200: apiserver uses a **watch cache**. The implementation can be found in\n201: `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/storage/cacher/`.\n202: \n203: *   **Initialization:** The cacher first performs a `LIST` to get the current state of all\n204:     objects and a `ResourceVersion` for that point-in-time. It then starts a `WATCH` from\n205:     that version to ensure a consistent stream of events.\n206: *   **Serving from Cache:** Most list and watch requests are served from this in-memory cache, which\n207:     dramatically reduces the load on etcd. Consistent reads are also served from the\n208:     cache. This is achieved by first fetching the revision number of the latest write from\n209:     etcd. The server then ensures the cache is at least that recent\u2014waiting for it to\n210:     refresh if necessary\u2014before serving the request.\n211: *   **Fallback to Storage:** If a client request cannot be served from the\n212:     cache's buffer, the request \"falls through\" to the underlying etcd storage.\n213: *   **Bookmarks:** The cacher uses bookmark events to track the latest `ResourceVersion` for\n214:     unchanged objects. This prevents the cache's `ResourceVersion` from becoming too old,\n215:     which avoids the need for expensive relist operations from etcd when the objects have\n216:     not been modified.\n217: \n218: ## 5. Conflict Resolution\n219: \n220: *   **Optimistic Concurrency via `resourceVersion`:** Clients are expected to perform updates using a\n221:     read-modify-write workflow. The apiserver uses the `resourceVersion` field of every\n222:     object to enforce optimistic concurrency. This `resourceVersion` is not an arbitrary number;\n223:     it maps directly to etcd's globally consistent `mod_revision`. When a client submits an\n224:     update (`PUT` or `PATCH`), it must provide the `resourceVersion` of the object it based its\n225:     modifications on. If the `resourceVersion` on the server does not match the current\n226:     `mod_revision` in etcd, the server rejects the request with a `409 Conflict` error. This\n227:     forces the client to re-read the object, resolve the conflict, and resubmit with the new\n228:     `resourceVersion`.\n229: *   **Server-Side Apply:** A declarative, \"intent-based\" patch. The server maintains a\n230:     `managedFields` section in the object's metadata to track which \"manager\" (e.g., a\n231:     controller) owns each field. This allows multiple actors to manage different parts of the\n232:     same object without overwriting each other's changes.\n233: \n234: ## 6. Discovery and OpenAPI\n235: \n236: Apiservers serve the `/apis` discovery endpoints and the `/openapi/v2` and `/openapi/v3`\n237: specifications. The generation of the OpenAPI specification is a multi-stage process.\n238: \n239: *   **`openapi-gen`**: This tool reflects on Go structs, reads godoc comments, and looks at\n240:     validation struct tags to generate a map of all API definitions.\n241: *   **`zz_generated.openapi.go`**: The output is a large Go file containing a\n242:     `GetOpenAPIDefinitions` function.\n243: *   **Runtime**: The `GenericAPIServer` calls this generated function to build the final OpenAPI\n244:     JSON spec that it serves to clients.\n245: \n246: ## 7. Security & Observability\n247: \n248: *   **Audit (`pkg/audit`):** The apiserver has a policy-driven event logging pipeline. The audit\n249:     policy controls what is logged and at which stage of a request.\n250: *   **Security:**\n251:     *   **mTLS:** The primary authentication mechanism for system components.\n252:     *   **Service Account Token Issuance:** The `kube-apiserver` acts as an OIDC provider,\n253:         issuing and validating JWTs for `ServiceAccount`s.\n254: \n255: ## 8. Streaming Protocols\n256: \n257: *   **Websockets:** The apiserver uses websockets to upgrade HTTP\n258:     connections for interactive, streaming protocols like `exec`, `attach`, and\n259:     `port-forward`. The `UpgradeAwareProxyHandler` manages this process.\n260: ",

May return nil if the requested object does not need metadata from\\n53: \\t// database. continueValue is optional and indicates that more results are available if the client\\n54: \\t// passes that value to the server in a subsequent call. remainingItemCount indicates the number\\n55: \\t// of remaining objects if the list is partial. For watch we should pass to helper.Watch().\\n67: \\t// Because resourceVersion is an opaque value, the default watch\\n68: \\t// behavior for non-zero watch is to watch the next value (if you pass\\n69: \\t// \\\"1\\\", you will see updates from \\\"2\\\" onwards).\\n70: \\tParseResourceVersion(resourceVersion string) (uint64, error)\\n71: }\\n72: \\n73: // ResponseMeta contains information about the database metadata that is associated with\\n74: // an object. \n167: // Interface offers a common interface for object marshaling/unmarshaling operations and\n168: // hides all the storage-related operations behind it.\n169: type Interface interface {\n170: \t// Returns Versioner associated with this interface.\n171: \tVersioner() Versioner\n172: \n173: \t// Create adds a new object at a key unless it already exists. 'ttl' is time-to-live\n174: \t// in seconds (0 means forever). If no error is returned and out is not nil, out will be\n175: \t// set to the read value from database.\n176: \tCreate(ctx context.Context, key string, obj, out runtime.Object, ttl uint64) error\n177: \n178: \t// Delete removes the specified key and returns the value that existed at that spot.\n179: \t// If key didn't exist, it will return NotFound storage error.\n180: \t// If 'cachedExistingObject' is non-nil, it can be used as a suggestion about the\n181: \t// current version of the object to avoid read operation from storage to get it.\n182: \t// However, the implementations have to retry in case suggestion is stale.\n183: \tDelete(\n184: \t\tctx context.Context, key string, out runtime.Object, preconditions *Preconditions,\n185: \t\tvalidateDeletion ValidateObjectFunc, cachedExistingObject runtime.Object, opts DeleteOptions) error\n186: \n187: \t// Watch begins watching the specified key. tryUpdate() if\n214: \t// other writers are simultaneously updating it, so tryUpdate() needs to take into account\n215: \t// the current contents of the object when deciding how the update object the eventual successful invocation of `tryUpdate` returns an output with the same serialized\n219: \t// contents as the input, it won't perform any update, but instead set `destination` to an object to get it.\n223: \t// However, the implementations have to retry in case suggestion is stale.\n224: \t//\n225: \t// Example:\n226: \t//\n227: \t// s := /* implementation of Interface */\n228: \t// err ShutdownInitiated: KILL signal received\\n31: - AfterShutdownDelayDuration: shutdown delay duration has passed\\n32: - InFlightRequestsDrained: all in flight request(s) have been drained\\n33: - HasBeenReady is signaled when the readyz endpoint succeeds for the first time\\n34: \\n35: The following is a sequence of shutdown events that we expect to see with\\n36:   'ShutdownSendRetryAfter' = false:\\n37: \\n38: T0: ShutdownInitiated: KILL signal received\\n39: \\t- /readyz starts returning red\\n40:     - run pre shutdown hooks\\n41: \\n42: T0+70s: AfterShutdownDelayDuration: shutdown delay duration has passed\\n43: \\t- the default value of 'ShutdownDelayDuration' is '70s'\\n44: \\t- it's time to initiate shutdown of the HTTP Server, server.Shutdown is invoked\\n45: \\t- as a consequene, the Close function has is called for all listeners\\n46:  \\t- the HTTP Server stops listening immediately\\n47: \\t- any new request arriving on a new TCP socket is denied with\\n48:       a network error similar to 'connection refused'\\n49:     - the HTTP Server waits gracefully for existing requests to complete\\n50:       up to '60s' (dictated by ShutdownTimeout)\\n51: \\t- active long running requests will receive a GOAWAY.\\n52: \\n53: T0+70s: HTTPServerStoppedListening:\\n54: \\t- this event is signaled when the HTTP Server has stopped listening\\n55:       which is immediately after server.Shutdown has been invoked\\n56: \\n57: T0 + 70s + up-to 60s: InFlightRequestsDrained: existing in flight requests have been drained\\n58: \\t- long running requests are outside of this scope\\n59: \\t- up-to 60s: the default value of 'ShutdownTimeout' is 60s, this means that\\n60:       any request in flight has a hard timeout of 60s.\\n61: \\t- it's time to call 'Shutdown' on the audit events since all\\n62: \\t  in flight request(s) have drained.\\n63: \\n64: \\n65: The following is a sequence of shutdown events that we expect to see with\\n66:   'ShutdownSendRetryAfter' = true:\\n67: \\n68: T0: ShutdownInitiated: KILL signal received\\n69: \\t- /readyz starts returning red\\n70:     - the HTTP Server waits gracefully for existing requests to complete\\n87:       up to '2s' (it's hard coded right now)\\n88: */\\n89: \\n90: // lifecycleSignal encapsulates a named apiserver event\\n91: type lifecycleSignal interface {\\n92: \\t// Signal signals the event, indicating that the event has occurred.\\n93: \\t// Signal is idempotent, once signaled the event stays signaled and\\n94: \\t// it immediately unblocks any goroutine waiting for this event.\\n95: \\tSignal()\\n96: \\n97: \\t// Signaled returns a channel that is closed when the underlying event\\n98: \\t// has been signaled. Successive calls to Signaled return the same value.\\n99: \\tSignaled() <-chan struct{}\\n100: \\n101: \\t// Name returns the name of the signal, useful for logging.\\n102: \\tName() string\\n103: }\\n104: \\n105: // lifecycleSignals provides an abstraction of the events that\\n106: // transpire during the lifecycle of the apiserver. This abstraction makes it easy\\n107: // for us to write unit tests that can verify expected graceful termination behavior.\\n108: //\\n109: // GenericAPIServer can use these to either:\\n110: //   - signal that a particular termination event has transpired\\n111: //   - wait for a designated termination event to transpire and do some action.\\n112: type lifecycleSignals struct {\\n113: \\t// ShutdownInitiated event is signaled when an apiserver shutdown has been initiated.\\n114: \\t// It is signaled when the `stopCh` provided by the main goroutine\\n115: \\t// receives a KILL signal and is closed as a consequence.\\n116: \\tShutdownInitiated lifecycleSignal\\n117: \\n118: \\t// AfterShutdownDelayDuration event is signaled as soon as ShutdownDelayDuration\\n119: \\t// has elapsed since the ShutdownInitiated event.\\n120: \\t// ShutdownDelayDuration allows the apiserver to delay shutdown for some time.\\n121: \\tAfterShutdownDelayDuration lifecycleSignal\\n122: \\n123: \\t// PreShutdownHooksStopped event is signaled when all registered\\n124: \\t// preshutdown hook(s) have finished running.\\n125: \\tPreShutdownHooksStopped lifecycleSignal\\n126: \\n127: \\t// NotAcceptingNewRequest event is signaled when the server is no\\n128: \\t// longer accepting any new request, from this point on any new\\n129: \\t// request will receive an error.\\n130: \\tNotAcceptingNewRequest lifecycleSignal\\n131: \\n132: \\t// InFlightRequestsDrained event is signaled when the existing requests\\n133: \\t// in flight have completed. This is used as signal to shut down the audit backends\\n134: \\tInFlightRequestsDrained lifecycleSignal\\n135: \\n136: \\t// HTTPServerStoppedListening termination event is signaled when the\\n137: \\t// HTTP Server has stopped listening to the underlying socket.\\n138: \\tHTTPServerStoppedListening lifecycleSignal\\n139: \\n140: \\t// HasBeenReady is signaled when the readyz endpoint succeeds for the first time.\\n141: \\tHasBeenReady lifecycleSignal\\n142: \\n143: \\t// MuxAndDiscoveryComplete is signaled when all known HTTP paths have been installed.\\n144: \\t// It exists primarily to avoid returning a 404 response when a resource actually exists but we haven't installed the path to a handler.\\n145: \\t// The actual logic is implemented by an APIServer using the generic server library.\\n146: \\tMuxAndDiscoveryComplete lifecycleSignal\\n147: }\\n148: \\n149: // ShuttingDown returns the lifecycle signal that is signaled when\\n150: // the server is not accepting any new requests.\\n151: // this is the lifecycle event that is exported to the request handler\\n152: // logic to indicate that the server is shutting down.\\n153: func (s lifecycleSignals) ShuttingDown() coordinate lifecycle of the apiserver\\n159: func newLifecycleSignals() lifecycleSignals {\\n160: \\treturn lifecycleSignals{\\n161: \\t\\tShutdownInitiated: newNamedChannelWrapper(\\\"InFlightRequestsDrained\\\"),\\n166: \\t\\tHTTPServerStoppedListening: newNamedChannelWrapper(\\\"HTTPServerStoppedListening\\\"),\\n167: \\t\\tHasBeenReady:               newNamedChannelWrapper(\\\"HasBeenReady\\\"),\\n168: \\t\\tMuxAndDiscoveryComplete:    newNamedChannelWrapper(\\\"MuxAndDiscoveryComplete\\\"),\\n169: \\t}\\n170: }\\n171: \\n172: func newNamedChannelWrapper(name string) lifecycleSignal {\\n173: \\treturn &namedChannelWrapper{\\n174: \\t\\tname: name,\\n175: \\t\\tonce: sync.Once{},\\n176: \\t\\tch:   make(chan struct{}),\\n177: \\t}\\n178: }\\n179: \\n180: type namedChannelWrapper struct {\\n181: \\tname string\\n182: \\tonce sync.Once\\n183: \\tch   chan struct{}\\n184: }\\n185: \\n186: func (e *namedChannelWrapper)   "content": "# Kubernetes API Server Library: Architecture and Extension Guide\n\n## 1. Subsystem Purpose and Boundaries\n\nThe `k8s.io/apiserver` library (`staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver`) is the foundational framework for building any Kubernetes-compatible API server. It is not tied to any specific set of resources; it provides the generic machinery\u2014request routing, authentication, authorization, admission, serialization, storage abstraction, and lifecycle management\u2014that any API server built on top of it inherits automatically.\n\n**Upstream dependencies** (what this library depends on):\n\n- `k8s.io/apimachinery` \u2014 core runtime types, `Scheme`, serialization codec interfaces, `GroupVersionKind`, `ResourceVersion` semantics.\n- `k8s.io/client-go` \u2014 informer factory, REST client configuration.\n- `k8s.io/component-base` \u2014 feature gates, metrics, tracing, logging.\n- `go.etcd.io/etcd/client/v3` \u2014 etcd storage backend.\n- `github.com/emicklei/go-restful/v3` \u2014 HTTP routing via `WebService` and `Container`.\n- `k8s.io/kube-openapi` \u2014 OpenAPI v2/v3 specification generation.\n\n**Downstream consumers** (who builds on top):\n\n- `cmd/kube-apiserver` \u2014 the main Kubernetes API server binary.\n- `k8s.io/kube-aggregator` \u2014 API aggregation layer (proxies to extension API servers).\n- `k8s.io/apiextensions-apiserver` \u2014 CRD controller.\n- Any third-party extension API server built with `sample-apiserver` or similar toolkits.\n\nThe library's boundary is crisp: it never knows about specific resources like `Pod` or `Deployment`. Those are registered by consumers through a well-defined `APIGroupInfo` struct.\n\n---\n\n## 2. Server Composition: The Delegation Chain\n\nThe `kube-apiserver` binary does not run a single server; it constructs a **chain of three `GenericAPIServer` instances** that delegate unhandled requests down the chain. This composition is assembled in `cmd/kube-apiserver/app/server.go` in `CreateServerChain`.\n\n```\n[Aggregator Server]  \u2192  [Kube API Server]  \u2192  [API Extensions Server]\n        \u2193                       \u2193                       \u2193\n  (apiregistration)        (core APIs)               (CRDs)\n  (proxy to external)    (apps, batch, \u2026)         (end of chain \u2192 404)\n```\n\nEach server is a `GenericAPIServer` (`staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/genericapiserver.go:110`) linked to the next via the `delegationTarget DelegationTarget` field:\n\n```go\n// genericapiserver.go:224\ndelegationTarget DelegationTarget\n```\n\nThe `DelegationTarget` interface (`genericapiserver.go:312`) requires implementations to expose post-start hooks, pre-shutdown hooks, health checks, and an `UnprotectedHandler()` that becomes the fallback HTTP handler for unrecognized paths. When a request arrives on the Aggregator Server and is not for an aggregated API service, its handler falls through to `delegationTarget.UnprotectedHandler()`, which is the Kube API Server's routing tree.\n\n**Termination of the chain** is an `emptyDelegate` (`genericapiserver.go:396`) whose `UnprotectedHandler()` returns `nil` \u2014 any request reaching it gets a 404.\n\n---\n\n## 3. Configuration and Server Construction\n\n### The `Config` \u2192 `CompletedConfig` \u2192 `GenericAPIServer` Pipeline\n\nAll server construction flows through a mandatory staged configuration pattern defined in `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/config.go`.\n\n**Stage 1: `Config` struct** (`config.go:112`) is a large struct capturing every tunable parameter. Key fields include:\n\n| Field | Purpose |\n|---|---|\n| `Authentication AuthenticationInfo` | Union authenticator chain |\n| `Authorization AuthorizationInfo` | Union authorizer chain |\n| `AdmissionControl admission.Interface` | Admission plugin chain |\n| `FlowControl utilflowcontrol.Interface` | Priority and Fairness (APF) controller |\n| `BuildHandlerChainFunc` | Hook to replace the entire handler chain |\n| `RESTOptionsGetter genericregistry.RESTOptionsGetter` | Supplies etcd config per resource |\n| `ResourceTransformers storagevalue.ResourceTransformers` | Encryption-at-rest transformer |\n| `MaxRequestsInFlight int` | Concurrency cap for non-mutating requests (default: 400) |\n| `MaxMutatingRequestsInFlight int` | Concurrency cap for mutating requests (default: 200) |\n| `RequestTimeout time.Duration` | Hard timeout for non-long-running requests (default: 60s) |\n| `ShutdownDelayDuration time.Duration` | Drain window before HTTP shutdown (default: 0, kube-apiserver sets 70s) |\n| `JSONPatchMaxCopyBytes int64` | Cap on JSON patch size accumulation (default: 3 MiB) |\n| `MaxRequestBodyBytes int64` | Hard limit on request body (default: 3 MiB) |\n\n`NewConfig()` (`config.go:397`) sets sane defaults and wires in `DefaultBuildHandlerChain` as the handler chain builder.\n\n**Stage 2: `Config.Complete(informers)`** (`config.go:714`) fills in derived fields \u2014 resolving the external address from `SecureServing`, completing OpenAPI security definitions, building the `EquivalentResourceRegistry`, and returning a `CompletedConfig`. This method enforces a programming contract: you cannot call `New()` on an incomplete config.\n\n**Stage 3: `completedConfig.New(name, delegationTarget)`** (`config.go:779`) instantiates the `GenericAPIServer`, wires all fields from the config, and registers a set of mandatory post-start hooks (informer start, APF config consumer, storage object count tracker).\n\n### The `RecommendedConfig` Shortcut\n\nExtension API server authors typically start with `RecommendedConfig` (`config.go:332`), which embeds `Config` and adds a `SharedInformerFactory` and `ClientConfig`. `RecommendedOptions` in `pkg/server/options/` translates command-line flags into a populated `RecommendedConfig`, covering TLS, authentication, authorization, audit, etcd, and admission.\n\n---\n\n## 4. The HTTP Handler Chain\n\nEvery request passes through the handler chain before reaching any REST endpoint. The canonical construction is `DefaultBuildHandlerChain` (`config.go:1028`). The chain is assembled inside-out: the innermost handler is the API router; each filter wraps the next one, so filters are applied in **reverse order of their appearance in `DefaultBuildHandlerChain`**.\n\nReading the actual construction (innermost first, i.e., the order requests flow through):\n\n```\nWithAuditInit                           \u2014 initialize audit context\nWithPanicRecovery                       \u2014 catch panics, return 500\nWithMuxAndDiscoveryComplete             \u2014 block 404s until all routes installed\nWithRequestReceivedTimestamp            \u2014 record wall-clock arrival time\nWithRequestInfo                         \u2014 parse URL \u2192 RequestInfo (resource, verb, namespace)\nWithRoutine (feature-gated)             \u2014 run subsequent handlers in goroutine\nWithHTTPLogging                         \u2014 log request/response details\nWithRetryAfter (if ShutdownSendRetryAfter) \u2014 429 during drain phase\nWithHSTS                                \u2014 add Strict-Transport-Security headers\nWithCacheControl                        \u2014 add Cache-Control: no-cache headers\nWithProbabilisticGoaway (if configured) \u2014 send HTTP/2 GOAWAY for LB rebalancing\nWithWatchTerminationDuringShutdown      \u2014 drain watch requests gracefully\nWithWaitGroup                           \u2014 track in-flight non-long-running requests\nWithRequestDeadline                     \u2014 enforce per-request deadline\nWithTimeoutForNonLongRunningRequests    \u2014 time out non-watch requests\nWithWarningRecorder                     \u2014 thread-safe Warning header accumulator\nWithCORS                                \u2014 handle CORS preflight\nWithAuthentication                      \u2014 identify the user (returns 401 on failure)\nWithTracing (feature-gated)             \u2014 start OpenTelemetry span\nWithAudit                               \u2014 log request to audit backend\nWithImpersonation / WithConstrainedImpersonation \u2014 handle user impersonation\nWithPriorityAndFairness / WithMaxInFlightLimit   \u2014 concurrency control\nWithAuthorization                       \u2014 check user is permitted (returns 403)\n[API Router]                            \u2014 dispatch to REST handler\n```\n\nCritical design decision: **authentication and authorization execute before the request body is deserialized**. The body is only decoded at admission time. This prevents parsing potentially malicious payloads before confirming identity and authorization.\n\nThe `BuildHandlerChainFunc` field in `Config` (`config.go:186`) allows complete replacement of this chain, which is how `kube-aggregator` wraps its own storage-version precondition check (`config.go:1024-1026`) before delegating to `DefaultBuildHandlerChain`.\n\n---\n\n## 5. API Group Registration\n\n### The `APIGroupInfo` Struct\n\nA consumer registers a set of API resources by constructing an `APIGroupInfo` and passing it to `GenericAPIServer.InstallAPIGroup()` or `InstallLegacyAPIGroup()`. The struct (`genericapiserver.go:72`) contains:\n\n```go\ntype APIGroupInfo struct {\n    PrioritizedVersions          []schema.GroupVersion\n    VersionedResourcesStorageMap map[string]map[string]rest.Storage\n    Scheme                       *runtime.Scheme\n    NegotiatedSerializer         runtime.NegotiatedSerializer\n    ParameterCodec               runtime.ParameterCodec\n    // ...\n}\n```\n\n`VersionedResourcesStorageMap` is a two-level map: `version \u2192 resource-path \u2192 rest.Storage`. For example, `\"v1\" \u2192 \"pods\" \u2192 podStorage`, `\"v1\" \u2192 \"pods/status\" \u2192 podStatusStorage`.\n\n### The `APIInstaller` and Route Registration\n\n`InstallAPIGroup` delegates to `APIInstaller.Install()` (`endpoints/installer.go:195`), which iterates the storage map in sorted order (for deterministic OpenAPI output) and calls `registerResourceHandlers` for each path. `registerResourceHandlers` (`installer.go:287`) type-asserts the `rest.Storage` against capability interfaces and installs HTTP routes accordingly:\n\n- `rest.Getter` \u2192 `GET /resource/{name}`\n- `rest.Lister` \u2192 `GET /resource`\n- `rest.Creater` \u2192 `POST /resource`\n- `rest.Updater` \u2192 `PUT /resource/{name}`\n- `rest.Patcher` \u2192 `PATCH /resource/{name}`\n- `rest.GracefulDeleter` \u2192 `DELETE /resource/{name}`\n- `rest.Watcher` \u2192 `GET /resource?watch=true`\n- `rest.Connecter` \u2192 `GET /resource/{name}/proxy`\n\nThis interface-based capability detection (`rest.go:53\u2013350`) is the primary extension point for selective verb support. A storage object need only implement the interfaces for the verbs it supports.\n\n### The `runtime.Scheme` as Type Registry\n\nThe `Scheme` serves multiple roles simultaneously:\n- **GVK \u2194 Go type mapping**: enables deserialization from wire format to internal type.\n- **Conversion**: hub-and-spoke between versions. All conversions go through the \"internal\" (hub) version. `conversion-gen` emits `zz_generated.conversion.go` files.\n- **Defaulting**: `defaulter-gen` emits `zz_generated.defaults.go`.\n- **Declarative validation**: registered via `zz_generated.validations.go`.\n\n---\n\n## 6. The `rest.Storage` and `genericregistry.Store`\n\n### The `rest.Storage` Interface Hierarchy\n\n`rest.Storage` (`registry/rest/rest.go:58`) is the minimal interface: `New()` and `Destroy()`. All capability interfaces compose with it optionally. There is no forced inheritance; the `APIInstaller` discovers capabilities via type assertions.\n\nThe `genericregistry.Store` struct (`registry/generic/registry/store.go:100`) is the standard implementation of `rest.StandardStorage` (which aggregates `Getter`, `Lister`, `Creater`, `Updater`, `GracefulDeleter`, `CollectionDeleter`, `Watcher`). Resource authors embed `Store` and override only what they need:\n\n```go\n// store.go:172-248 \u2014 key extension hooks on Store\nCreateStrategy  rest.RESTCreateStrategy   // PrepareForCreate, Validate, Canonicalize\nUpdateStrategy  rest.RESTUpdateStrategy   // PrepareForUpdate, ValidateUpdate\nDeleteStrategy  rest.RESTDeleteStrategy   // DefaultGarbageCollectionPolicy\nBeginCreate     BeginCreateFunc           // transaction-like commit/revert hook\nAfterCreate     AfterCreateFunc           // post-create side effects\nBeginUpdate     BeginUpdateFunc\nAfterUpdate     AfterUpdateFunc\nAfterDelete     AfterDeleteFunc\nDecorator       func(runtime.Object)      // exit hook after storage read\n```\n\nThe `CreateStrategy`, `UpdateStrategy`, and `DeleteStrategy` are Go interfaces implemented per resource type (e.g., `pkg/registry/core/pod/strategy.go`). They enforce resource-specific invariants \u2014 name generation, field defaulting, cross-field validation \u2014 that are distinct from the schema-level validation in admission plugins.\n\n### The `DryRunnableStorage` Wrapper\n\n`Store.Storage` is typed as `DryRunnableStorage` (`store.go:221`), not `storage.Interface` directly. This wrapper intercepts writes when `DryRun` is set on the request options, logging what would have happened without persisting to etcd. The `StorageDecorator` function type (`generic/storage_decorator.go:29`) allows the caching layer to be injected: the default decorator wraps the raw etcd store in a `Cacher`.\n\n---\n\n## 7. The Storage Stack: From Request to etcd\n\nThe storage path from `Store` to etcd traverses three layers:\n\n```\ngenericregistry.Store\n        \u2193 (DryRunnableStorage)\nstorage/cacher.Cacher          \u2190 watch cache + in-memory read path\n        \u2193 (storage.Interface)\nstorage/etcd3.store             \u2190 gRPC calls to etcd cluster\n```\n\n### The `storage.Interface`\n\nDefined in `storage/interfaces.go:169`, this interface has seven key methods:\n\n| Method | Semantics |\n|---|---|\n| `Create` | Atomic conditional create; fails if key exists |\n| `Delete` | Delete by key with optional preconditions |\n| `Watch` | Stream events from a given `ResourceVersion` |\n| `Get` | Point read with `ResourceVersion` constraint |\n| `GetList` | Prefix scan with pagination (`continue` token) |\n| `GuaranteedUpdate` | Read-modify-write loop until etcd CAS succeeds |\n| `GetCurrentResourceVersion` | Issues empty list, extracts revision |\n\nThe `GuaranteedUpdate` method (`interfaces.go:243`) is the mechanism behind optimistic concurrency: it retries the `tryUpdate` callback until the etcd compare-and-swap succeeds. The `resourceVersion` field on every object maps directly to etcd's `mod_revision`.\n\n### The Watch Cache (`storage/cacher`)\n\nThe `Cacher` (`cacher/cacher.go`) sits between `Store` and etcd:\n\n- **Initialization**: performs a full `GetList` from etcd to seed the in-memory `watchCache`, capturing the `ResourceVersion` at that point.\n- **Continuous sync**: establishes a persistent `Watch` from that `ResourceVersion`, updating the in-memory store for every event.\n- **Serving reads**: most `List` and `Watch` calls are answered from the in-memory cache. Consistent reads are satisfied by first fetching the latest revision from etcd and then waiting for the cache to advance to that revision.\n- **Buffer fallback**: if the event ring buffer is too small to satisfy a historical watch request, the cacher falls through to the underlying etcd store.\n- **Bookmarks**: the cacher sends periodic bookmark events (frequency: `defaultBookmarkFrequency = 1 minute`, `cacher.go:76`) to advance the `ResourceVersion` of watchers even when objects are unchanged. This prevents expensive relist operations after reconnection.\n- **Encryption**: the `ResourceTransformers` (`storagevalue.ResourceTransformers`) operate at the etcd3 store layer, transforming values on read and write (enabling AES-CBC, AES-GCM, or KMS envelope encryption).\n\n---\n\n## 8. Admission Control\n\nAdmission is a two-phase chain defined in `admission/interfaces.go`:\n\n```go\ntype MutationInterface interface {\n    Admit(ctx context.Context, a Attributes, o ObjectInterfaces) error\n}\n\ntype ValidationInterface interface {\n    Validate(ctx context.Context, a Attributes, o ObjectInterfaces) error\n}\n```\n\nThe `chainAdmissionHandler` (`admission/chain.go:23`) implements both: it iterates all plugins and calls `Admit` on each mutator, then `Validate` on each validator. **Mutations must complete before validations run** \u2014 this is enforced by the `Store`'s create and update paths, which call the mutation chain, then the validation chain.\n\nKey design decisions:\n- **Body deserialization happens here, not earlier**: the `Attributes` passed to plugins (`admission/interfaces.go:31`) contains the decoded `runtime.Object`. Plugins that do not need the body (e.g., webhook plugins that call an external service) still receive it.\n- **Re-invocation**: mutating webhooks can request re-invocation if another mutating plugin later modifies a field they care about. The `ReinvocationContext` (`admission/interfaces.go:107`) tracks this state.\n- **Plugin initialization**: `PluginInitializer` (`admission/interfaces.go:159`) injects shared dependencies (informers, authorizer, client) into plugins at startup. Extension admission plugins must implement the appropriate `PluginInitializer` interfaces or use `admission/initializer`.\n- **Dry-run**: the `IsDryRun()` method on `Attributes` (`admission/interfaces.go:52`) signals to plugins with external side effects that they must not modify state.\n\n---\n\n## 9. Concurrency Control: Two Mechanisms\n\n### 9.1 Simple MaxInFlight (`server/filters/maxinflight.go`)\n\nThe fallback when APF is disabled. Separate counters for read and mutating requests, bounded by `MaxRequestsInFlight` and `MaxMutatingRequestsInFlight`. Requests exceeding the limit receive `HTTP 429 Too Many Requests`. A periodic goroutine logs watermark metrics.\n\n### 9.2 Priority and Fairness (`pkg/util/flowcontrol`)\n\nWhen `FlowControl` is non-nil in `Config`, `DefaultBuildHandlerChain` wraps requests with `WithPriorityAndFairness` instead. APF classifies each request into a `FlowSchema` and `PriorityLevel`, then queues or dispatches it based on configured concurrency shares. This prevents a single busy client (e.g., a misbehaving controller) from starving critical system operations. The APF state is driven by a `FlowControlAPI` watch loop started as a post-start hook (`config.go:937`).\n\n---\n\n## 10. Server Lifecycle and Graceful Termination\n\nThe `lifecycleSignals` struct (`server/lifecycle_signals.go:112`) models the shutdown state machine as a set of named, one-shot channels:\n\n```\nShutdownInitiated\n    \u2514\u2500 AfterShutdownDelayDuration (after ShutdownDelayDuration elapses)\n        \u2514\u2500 NotAcceptingNewRequest\n            \u2514\u2500 InFlightRequestsDrained (after all non-long-running requests complete)\n                \u2514\u2500 HTTPServerStoppedListening\n```\n\nShutdown sequence (default, `ShutdownSendRetryAfter=false`):\n1. SIGTERM closes `stopCh` \u2192 `ShutdownInitiated` fires, `/readyz` fails immediately.\n2. Pre-shutdown hooks run (e.g., controller-manager drains).\n3. After `ShutdownDelayDuration` (kube-apiserver default: 70s), `AfterShutdownDelayDuration` fires.\n4. `http.Server.Shutdown()` is called \u2014 the listener closes, existing requests are given up to `ShutdownTimeout` (default: 60s) to complete.\n5. Once in-flight requests drain, `InFlightRequestsDrained` fires; the audit backend shuts down.\n\nWith `ShutdownSendRetryAfter=true`, the HTTP listener stays open after the delay but responds to new requests with `HTTP 429` and `Retry-After: 1` (via `WithRetryAfter`, `config.go:1094`). This allows clients to reconnect to a healthy instance while in-flight requests complete on this one.\n\n---\n\n## 11. Extension Points and Customization Hooks\n\n### 11.1 Handler Chain Replacement (`BuildHandlerChainFunc`)\n\nThe most powerful extension point. Replacing `Config.BuildHandlerChainFunc` allows inserting arbitrary HTTP middleware or completely changing the chain structure. Risk: incorrect ordering can silently break authentication or authorization (e.g., placing a mutating handler before `WithAuthentication` means unauthenticated code runs).\n\n### 11.2 Custom `rest.Storage` Implementations\n\nImplement only the `rest.*` interfaces your resource supports. For resources that need custom backend logic (e.g., status subresources that trigger external actions), implement `rest.Storage` directly without embedding `genericregistry.Store`. The `APIInstaller` discovers all capabilities via type assertion at registration time.\n\n### 11.3 Admission Plugins\n\nRegister via `admission.PluginInitializer` and `admission.Registry`. Both in-tree and out-of-tree webhooks (via `MutatingWebhookConfiguration` and `ValidatingWebhookConfiguration`) register as plugins in the chain. Out-of-tree plugins written in Go embed into a custom binary; webhooks communicate via HTTP with the external service.\n\n**Risk**: mutating webhooks that modify fields owned by other webhooks can trigger loops. The `ReinvocationContext` bounds this to one extra pass per object.\n\n### 11.4 Storage Transformer (Encryption at Rest)\n\n`Config.ResourceTransformers` (`config.go:222`) injects a per-resource value transformer at the etcd3 layer. This is how `EncryptionConfiguration` (KMS, AES-CBC, AES-GCM) is applied. Custom transformers can be injected for testing or non-standard encryption schemes.\n\n### 11.5 Post-Start and Pre-Shutdown Hooks\n\n`Config.AddPostStartHook(name, func)` (`config.go:596`) registers functions that run concurrently after the server starts listening. `Config.AddPreShutdownHook` registers cleanup to run before the drain phase. These are the canonical mechanisms for resource-specific initialization (warming caches, establishing leases, starting controllers) without modifying the generic server.\n\n### 11.6 `StorageDecorator` (Watch Cache Bypass)\n\n`generic.StorageDecorator` (`generic/storage_decorator.go:29`) is a function type that the `Store` uses to create its underlying `storage.Interface`. The default implementation wraps etcd in the watch cache. Replacing it with `generic.UndecoratedStorage` bypasses the cache entirely \u2014 useful for resources like `Events` that have different consistency requirements.\n\n### 11.7 `APIService` and Aggregated API Servers\n\nFor extensions requiring a completely custom storage backend, protocol, or non-CRUD verbs, deploy a separate API server and register an `APIService` object. The Aggregator Server proxies matching requests to the extension server. The extension server itself typically embeds `GenericAPIServer` from this library.\n\n---\n\n## 12. Operational Implications\n\n### Performance\n\n**Watch cache sizing**: the cacher's event ring buffer defaults to `storageWatchListPageSize = 10000` events (`cacher.go:65`). For resources with very high change rates (e.g., `Events`), the buffer can overflow, forcing expensive relists from etcd. Monitor the `apiserver_watch_cache_events_dispatched_total` metric.\n\n**Concurrency limits**: the default `MaxRequestsInFlight=400` and `MaxMutatingRequestsInFlight=200` are conservative. Clusters with many controllers and large node counts routinely saturate these limits, triggering 429s. APF (`FlowControl`) is the recommended replacement \u2014 it shapes by `FlowSchema` rather than globally rejecting, but requires careful tuning of `PriorityLevel` concurrency shares.\n\n**Admission latency**: every mutating request waits for all admission plugins synchronously. Slow webhooks (e.g., those with cold-start external calls) add directly to P99 write latency. Admission webhook timeouts (`failurePolicy: Fail`) can also convert webhook unavailability into write failures for the entire cluster.\n\n**Serialization overhead**: JSON serialization and deserialization happen on every request, including for large object lists. Enable protobuf for internal clients (controller-manager, scheduler) \u2014 the library supports it via `runtime.ContentTypeProtobuf`. CBOR support is gated behind `CBORServingAndStorage` feature gate.\n\n### Reliability\n\n**Optimistic concurrency**: `GuaranteedUpdate` retries indefinitely until the etcd CAS succeeds. Under very high write contention on a single object (e.g., a heavily patched ConfigMap), retry storms can inflate latency significantly. Server-Side Apply reduces this by merging field-level ownership rather than replacing the whole object.\n\n**Graceful shutdown tuning**: the 70-second default `ShutdownDelayDuration` in kube-apiserver gives load balancers time to drain before the listener closes. Too short a delay causes requests to new TCP connections to fail during rolling restarts. Too long a delay extends the time a partially-degraded instance serves traffic.\n\n**etcd compaction and watch cache**: if etcd compacts a revision that a watcher needs, the watch fails with `ErrCompacted`. The cacher must relist and re-establish the watch. Under heavy etcd compaction (or slow cachers), this causes temporary inconsistency in the watch cache. Bookmark events (`ProgressNotify`, `interfaces.go:326`) mitigate this by advancing watchers proactively.\n\n### Maintainability\n\n**Code generation dependency**: the API registration pipeline relies heavily on generated code (`zz_generated.conversion.go`, `zz_generated.deepcopy.go`, `zz_generated.defaults.go`, `zz_generated.validations.go`, `zz_generated.openapi.go`). Any change to API types requires regeneration. These files are checked in; stale generated files produce subtle bugs (missing conversions, wrong OpenAPI spec).\n\n**Hub-and-spoke conversion**: adding a new API version requires implementing conversion functions to/from the internal hub version, not directly between external versions. This keeps the number of conversion functions `O(n)` rather than `O(n\u00b2)`, but means the internal type must be a superset of all external versions.\n\n**Feature gates**: new behaviors are gated behind `featuregate.FeatureGate` entries in `pkg/features`. This allows gradual rollout but creates configuration surface that must be tested in all combinations. The `EffectiveVersion` and `EmulationVersion` fields in `Config` allow a binary built at version N to emulate API availability at version N-1 for controlled upgrades.\n\n---\n\n## 13. File and Directory Reference\n\n| Path | Role |\n|---|---|\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/ARCHITECTURE.md` | Official architecture narrative |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/config.go` transformer interface and implementations |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/options/` | `RecommendedOptions`, flag-to-config translation |\n| `cmd/kube-apiserver/app/server.go` | `CreateServerChain` \u2014 assembles the three-server delegation chain |\n",
  "file_path": "/workspace/documentation.md"
